<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Cinemax</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p><b>Cinemax</b> (sometimes abbreviated as <b>Max</b>) is an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> <a href="/wiki/Pay_television" title="Pay television">pay</a> <a href="/wiki/Cable_television" title="Cable television">cable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_network" title="Television network">television network</a> that is owned by the <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">Home Box Office Inc.</a> <a href="/wiki/Operating_subsidiary" title="Operating subsidiary">operating subsidiary</a> of <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a>. Cinemax primarily broadcasts theatrically released <a href="/wiki/Feature_film" title="Feature film">feature films</a>, along with original series, <a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography">softcore pornographic</a> series and films, <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentaries</a> and special behind-the-scenes features.</p>
<p>As of July 2015, Cinemax's programming is available to approximately 21.325 million television households (18.3% of cable, satellite and telco customers) in the United States (20.785 million subscribers or 17.9% of all households with pay television service receive at least Cinemax's primary channel).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cinemax launched on August 1, 1980<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a>'s answer to <a href="/wiki/The_Movie_Channel" title="The Movie Channel">The Movie Channel</a> (which at the time, was owned by <a href="/wiki/Warner-Amex_Satellite_Entertainment" title="Warner-Amex Satellite Entertainment">Warner-Amex Satellite Entertainment</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Joint_venture" title="Joint venture">joint venture</a> between Time Warner predecessor <a href="/wiki/Warner_Communications" title="Warner Communications">Warner Communications</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Express" title="American Express">American Express</a>; TMC is now owned by the <a href="/wiki/Showtime_Networks" title="Showtime Networks">Showtime Networks</a> subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/CBS_Corporation" title="CBS Corporation">CBS Corporation</a> – previously under <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(original)" title="Viacom (original)">Viacom</a> from 1983 to 2005). Cinemax was originally owned by <a href="/wiki/Time_Inc." title="Time Inc.">Time-Life Inc.</a>, which later merged with Warner Communications in 1989 to form the present-day Time Warner.</p>
<p>Unlike HBO – and most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cable_network" title="Cable network">cable</a> and broadcast channels already on the air at the time of its launch – Cinemax had broadcast a 24-hour-a-day schedule from its sign-on (HBO ran only nine hours of programming a day from 3:00 p.m. to midnight <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">Eastern Time</a> until September 1981, when it adopted a 24-hour weekend schedule that ran until midnight Eastern Time on Sunday nights; it implemented the round-the-clock schedule on weekdays as well on December 28 of that year). On-air spokesman <a href="/wiki/Robert_Culp" title="Robert Culp">Robert Culp</a> told viewers that Cinemax would be about movies, and nothing but movies. At the time, HBO featured a wider range of programming, including some entertainment news interstitials, <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentaries</a>, children's programming, sporting events and <a href="/wiki/Television_special" title="Television special">television specials</a> (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> plays, <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_comedy" title="Stand-up comedy">stand-up comedy</a> acts and <a href="/wiki/Concert" title="Concert">concerts</a>). Movie classics were a mainstay of Cinemax at its birth, presented "all uncut and commercial-free" as Culp said on-air. A heavy schedule of films from the 1950s to the 1970s made up most of the channel's program schedule.</p>
<p>Cinemax succeeded in its early years because cable television subscribers typically had access to only about three dozen channels due to system <a href="/wiki/Cable_television_headend" title="Cable television headend">headends</a> at the time of Cinemax's debut were capable of carrying only a <a href="/wiki/Channel_capacity" title="Channel capacity">limited number of channels</a>. Movies were the most sought-after program category among cable subscribers at the time, and that Cinemax would show classic films without commercial interruptions and editing for time and content made the channel an attractive add-on for HBO subscribers. In many cases, cable providers would not sell Cinemax to customers who did not already have a subscription to HBO. The two channels were typically sold as a package, and were usually offered at a discount for subscribers that chose to get both channels. The typical pricing for a monthly subscription to HBO in the early 1980s was <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>12.95 per month, while Cinemax typically could be added for between US$7 and $10 extra per month.</p>
<p>In 1983, Time-Life Inc. filed a federal <a href="/wiki/Trademark_infringement" title="Trademark infringement">trademark infringement</a> lawsuit against <a href="/wiki/Independent_station_(North_America)" title="Independent station (North America)">independent station</a> <a href="/wiki/KOKI-TV" title="KOKI-TV">KOKI-TV</a> (now a <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> affiliate) in <a href="/wiki/Tulsa,_Oklahoma" title="Tulsa, Oklahoma">Tulsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> and its owners Tulsa 23, Ltd. over the use of the slogan "We Are Your Movie Star", which both the <a href="/wiki/Television_station" title="Television station">television station</a> and Cinemax were using as their slogans at that time; the suit went into proceedings in an Oklahoma <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_District_Court" title="Federal District Court">Federal District Court</a>, Cinemax lost the case. As additional movie-oriented channels launched on cable television, Cinemax began to change its programming philosophy in order to maintain its subscriber base. First, the channel opted to schedule R-rated movies during daytime slots (HBO would only show R-rated movies during the nighttime hours, after 8:00 p.m. Eastern Time, a policy that network largely continues to adhere to as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>); Cinemax then decided it could compete by airing more <a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography">adult-oriented movies</a> that contained <a href="/wiki/Nudity" title="Nudity">nudity</a> and depictions of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a>, launching the weekly "Friday After Dark" late-night block in 1984 (which also featured the short-lived adult drama <i>Scandals</i>, and a series of <a href="/wiki/Anthology_series" title="Anthology series">anthology specials</a> under the <i>Eros America</i> and <i>Eros International</i> banners).</p>
<p>During the network's first decade on the air, Cinemax had also aired some original music programming: during the mid-to-late 1980s, upon the meteoric rise in popularity of <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>, Cinemax began airing <a href="/wiki/Music_video" title="Music video">music videos</a> in the form of an <a href="/wiki/Interstitial_program" title="Interstitial program">interstitial</a> that ran during extended breaks between films called <i>MaxTrax</i>; it also ran music specials under the banner <i>Cinemax Sessions</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> as well as the music interview and performance series <i>Album Flash</i> during that same time period. The mid- and late-1980s also saw the addition of a limited amount of series programming onto Cinemax's schedule including the <a href="/wiki/Sketch_comedy" title="Sketch comedy">sketch comedy</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Second_City_Television" title="Second City Television">Second City Television</a></i> (whose U.S. broadcast rights were acquired by the channel from <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> in 1983) and the <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Max_Headroom_(TV_series)" title="Max Headroom (TV series)">Max Headroom</a></i> (which had also aired on <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> from 1987 to 1988). Comedy specials were also occasionally broadcast on the channel during the late 1980s, under the <i>Cinemax Comedy Experiment</i> banner, featuring free-form <a href="/wiki/Sketch_comedy" title="Sketch comedy">sketch</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Improvisational_comedy" title="Improvisational comedy">improvisational</a> styles from various rising and established <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_comedy" title="Stand-up comedy">stand-up comics</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Howie_Mandel" title="Howie Mandel">Howie Mandel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Elliott" title="Chris Elliott">Chris Elliott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Bogosian" title="Eric Bogosian">Eric Bogosian</a>). Although its programming had diversified, Cinemax had foremost remained a movie channel. In February 1988, the network premiere broadcast of the 1987 action-comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Lethal_Weapon" title="Lethal Weapon">Lethal Weapon</a></i> became the highest rated telecast in Cinemax's history at that time, averaging a 16.9 rating and 26 share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1990, Cinemax limited its programming lineup mainly to movies. However starting in 1992, Cinemax re-entered into television series development with the addition of adult-oriented scripted series similar in content to the softcore pornographic films featured on the channel in late night (such as the network's first original adult series <i>Erotic Confessions</i>, and later series entries such as <i>Hot Line</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Passion_Cove" title="Passion Cove">Passion Cove</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lingerie_(TV_series)" title="Lingerie (TV series)">Lingerie</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Co-Ed_Confidential" title="Co-Ed Confidential">Co-Ed Confidential</a></i>), marking a return to adult series for the channel.</p>
<p>From 1992 to 1997, Cinemax aired daily movie showcases in set timeslots, centering on a certain genre which differed each day of the week; with the introduction of a new on-air presentation package in 1993, the genre of a given showcase was represented by various <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictograms</a> that usually appeared within a specialized feature presentation bumper before the start of the movie;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinemax_makeover_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cinemax_makeover-6">[6]</a></sup> the symbols included: "Comedy" (represented by an abstract face made up of various movie props, with an open mouth made to appear like it is laughing),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> "Suspense" (represented by a running man silhouette within a jagged film strip), "Premiere" (represented by an <a href="/wiki/Exclamation_mark" title="Exclamation mark">exclamation mark</a> immersed in spotlights), "Horror" (represented by a skull augmented with a devil horn and a gear-shaped eye, overlaid in front on a <a href="/wiki/Casket" title="Casket">casket</a>), "Drama" (represented by abstract comedy and tragedy masks),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> "Vanguard" (represented by a <a href="/wiki/Globe" title="Globe">globe</a> overlaid on a film strip), "Action" (represented by a <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">machine gun</a> and an explosion) and "Classic" (represented by a classic movie-era couple embracing and kissing). The particular film genre that played on the specific day (and time) varied by country.</p>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:68%;">

<li>Thursday, 8:00 p.m. Eastern Time: <b>Drama</b> (originally <b>Horror</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polishes_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-polishes-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>Friday (originally Wednesday),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polishes_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-polishes-9">[9]</a></sup> 8:00 p.m. Eastern Time: <b>Premiere</b></li>
<li>Saturday, 10:00 p.m. (originally 11:30 p.m.) Eastern Time:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polishes_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-polishes-9">[9]</a></sup> <b>Action</b></li>

</div>

</div>
<p>These genre-based movie presentations ended in September 1997, as part of an extensive <a href="/wiki/Rebranding" title="Rebranding">rebranding</a> of the network; Cinemax's only themed movie presentations at that point became a nightly featured movie at 8:00 p.m. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">Eastern Time</a> (under the branding "Max Hits at 8") and a nightly primetime movie at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time (branded as "Max Prime at 10").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaxMovieFan_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaxMovieFan-10">[10]</a></sup> Upon the launch of the two multiplex channels in 1998, Cinemax offered "sneak preview" blocks of programs that could be seen on ActionMax and ThrillerMax in primetime, respectively on Saturdays and Sundays. By the mid-2000s, classic films released from the 1940s to the 1970s – which had been a mainstay of the Cinemax schedule from its launch (and continued to air on the main channel in the morning hours during the 1990s and early 2000s) – were relegated to some of its multiplex channels, and became prominent on its multiplex service, 5StarMax. Today, a large majority of mainstream films featured on the main channel are releases from the 1990s to the present, with some films from the 1970s and 1980s included on the schedule.</p>
<p>In 2001, Cinemax began to shift its focus from solely airing second-run feature films that were previously broadcast on sister channel HBO before their Cinemax debut, to premiering select blockbuster and lesser-known theatrical films before their initial broadcast on HBO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In February 2011, Cinemax announced that it would begin offering mainstream original programming (in the form of action-themed series aimed at men between the ages of 18 and 49) to compete with sister channel HBO, and rivals <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_channel)" title="Showtime (TV channel)">Showtime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Starz" title="Starz">Starz</a> – as well as due to competition from other movie services such as <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a>; these programs were also added in an effort to change the longstanding image of Cinemax as a channel mostly known for carrying softcore pornographic series and movies (although its adult programming continues to appear as part of the channel's late night schedule).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Channels">Channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_channels">List of channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: List of channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>ActionMax redirects here. For the 1980s video game system, see <a href="/wiki/Action_Max" title="Action Max">Action Max</a>.</i></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For the UK channel formerly known as ActionMax, see <a href="/wiki/Movies4Men_2" title="Movies4Men 2">Movies4Men 2</a>.</div>
<p><i>MovieMax redirects here. For the Canadian premium movie service formerly known as MovieMax!, see <a href="/wiki/Encore_Avenue" title="Encore Avenue">Encore Avenue</a>.</i> <i>5StarMax redirects here. It is not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/5Star" title="5Star">5Star</a>.</i></p>
<p>Depending on the service provider, Cinemax provides up to fifteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiplex_(TV)" title="Multiplex (TV)">multiplex channels</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> – eight 24-hour multiplex channels, all of which are simulcast in both <a href="/wiki/Standard-definition_television" title="Standard-definition television">standard definition</a> and <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high definition</a> – as well as a <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">subscription video-on-demand</a> service (Cinemax On Demand). Cinemax broadcasts its primary and multiplex channels on both Eastern and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Time_Zone" title="Pacific Time Zone">Pacific Time Zone</a> schedules. The respective coastal feeds of each channel are usually packaged together (though most cable providers only offer the east and west coast feeds of the main Cinemax channel), resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_time_zones_on_North_American_broadcasting" title="Effects of time zones on North American broadcasting">difference in local airtimes</a> for a particular movie or program between two geographic locations being three hours at most.</p>
<p>HBO, which is also owned by Time Warner, operates as a separate service – and although Cinemax and HBO are very frequently sold together as a singular package – subscribers to one of the services do not necessarily have to subscribe to the other.</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="background-color: #FFFFFF; text-align: center;" width="100%">
<tr style="color:white;">
<th style="background-color: #8B0000">Channel</th>

The channel commonly premieres new movies – debuting on the channel within a lag of between eight months to one year on average from their initial theatrical release – on Saturday nights at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time as part of "See It Saturday", and broadcasts a featured movie Sunday through Thursdays at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time. Cinemax also runs original action series on Friday evenings at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time.</td>

The service broadcasts a featured movie every night at 9:00 p.m. Eastern Time. MoreMax was originally named <b>"Cinemax 2"</b> until 1998.</td>

The channel broadcasts a featured classic every night at 9:00 p.m. Eastern Time. It is the only Cinemax channel that does not air Max After Dark content.</td>

ActionMax replaced <b>"Cinemax 3"</b>, which existed from 1995 to 1998.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td align="center" width="200pt"><br/>
<b>Cinemáx</b><br/>

The channel originally launched on May 17, 2001 as WMax,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plex_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plex-14">[14]</a></sup> which was targeted at a female audience, and featured dramas, mysteries and classic romance films.</td>

<td align="left">Launched in 1998, ThrillerMax runs mystery, suspense, horror and thriller movies; the channel runs a prime time film block, "When the Clock Strikes 10", showing a different featured mystery, suspense or thriller at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time seven nights a week.</td>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1991, HBO and Cinemax became the first premium services to offer multiplex channels to cable subscribers as companions to the main network. These additional services of the two premium channels were initially made available to TeleCable systems in <a href="/wiki/Racine,_Wisconsin" title="Racine, Wisconsin">Racine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>; <a href="/wiki/Overland_Park,_Kansas" title="Overland Park, Kansas">Overland Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a> suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Richardson,_Texas" title="Richardson, Texas">Richardson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plano,_Texas" title="Plano, Texas">Plano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The following year, research compiled by <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_Media_Research" title="Nielsen Media Research">Nielsen Media Research</a> showed that multiplex delivery of HBO and Cinemax resulted in a positive impact on subscriber usage and attitudes towards their service, including increasing subscriber retention of pay cable subscriptions. The first Cinemax multiplex channel, Cinemax 2, was launched on these three systems; a third channel, Cinemax 3, would eventually make its debut in 1995.</p>
<p>The first major expansion to the multiplex came in 1998, with the rebranding of one multiplex channel and the launch of two additional channels as genre-based services: Cinemax 2 underwent a rebrand under the new name MoreMax, while Cinemax 3 was replaced by ActionMax (maintaining a focus on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Action_films" title="Action films">action</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adventure_film" title="Adventure film">adventure films</a>); ThrillerMax (which features mystery, <a href="/wiki/Thriller_film" title="Thriller film">suspense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">horror films</a>) also made its debut as a newly created channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Four additional themed channels were launched on May 17, 2001: OuterMax (which carried films dealing with the <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, horror and <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> genres), Wmax (a channel featuring films that appeal toward a female audience), @Max (featuring films aimed at 18- to 34-year-olds) and 5StarMax (focusing on critically acclaimed and classic feature films).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plex_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Plex-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Four_Plexes_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Four_Plexes-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 1, 2013, WMax and @Max were respectively relaunched as MovieMax and Max Latino. Max Latino – which dropped its distinct branding and was renamed Cinemáx on April 1, 2015 – mirrors the schedule of the flagship Cinemax channel (similar to the format of HBO Latino, which simulcasts most of the HBO schedule except for certain differing programs), featuring Spanish-language dubs of feature films and original series broadcast by the main channel. MovieMax started out as a family-oriented channel which did not broadcast R-rated films, and focuses on recent and classic hit movies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Cinemax multiplex was collectively known as "MultiMax" (or alternately "MultiMax from Cinemax") for several years beginning with the 1998 expansion. As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, however, the channels are not known under an "official" marketed name (however, HBO and Cinemax's respective multiplex packages are referred collectively by certain providers as the "HBO/MAX Pak").</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_services">Other services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinemax_HD">Cinemax HD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cinemax HD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Cinemax HD</b> is a <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high definition</a> simulcast feed of Cinemax that broadcasts in the <a href="/wiki/1080i" title="1080i">1080i</a> resolution format. In addition to its main channel, Cinemax also operates high definition simulcast feeds of all seven multiplex channels. The flagship network began transmitting its programming exclusively in high definition on September 1, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Cinemax HD is available on major cable, <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fiber optic</a> and satellite providers such as <a href="/wiki/Dish_Network" title="Dish Network">Dish Network</a>, <a href="/wiki/DirecTV" title="DirecTV">DirecTV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner_Cable" title="Time Warner Cable">Time Warner Cable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cox_Communications" title="Cox Communications">Cox Communications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comcast_Cable" title="Comcast Cable">Comcast Xfinity</a>, <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_U-verse" title="AT&amp;T U-verse">AT&amp;T U-verse</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verizon_FiOS" title="Verizon FiOS">Verizon FiOS</a>, although few providers offer all eight multiplex channels in HD.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinemax_On_Demand">Cinemax On Demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cinemax On Demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cinemax operates a subscription video-on-demand service, <b>Cinemax On Demand</b>, which is available at no additional charge to new and existing Cinemax subscribers. The Cinemax On Demand service, which launched in 2002,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> offers program content available in standard or high definition including feature films, episodes of Cinemax's original action series, adult programming and special behind-the-scenes features including <a href="/wiki/Film_promotion" title="Film promotion">interviews</a>. Cinemax on Demand's rotating program selection incorporates select new titles that are added each Friday, alongside existing program titles held over from the previous one to two weeks.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Max_GO">Max GO</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Max GO">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaxGO.svg"><img alt="MaxGO.svg" data-file-height="302" data-file-width="800" height="47" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/MaxGO.svg/125px-MaxGO.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/MaxGO.svg/188px-MaxGO.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/MaxGO.svg/250px-MaxGO.svg.png 2x" width="125"/></a></div>
<p>On September 13, 2010, Cinemax launched <b>Max GO</b>, a website which features more than 700 hours of content available for streaming in standard or high definition, at no additional charge to Cinemax subscribers. Content available on the service includes feature films, documentaries, and late night adult programming featured on Cinemax's Max After Dark block.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> It is available to Cinemax subscribers of AT&amp;T U-verse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Cox Communications,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> DirecTV,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Dish Network,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Suddenlink_Communications" title="Suddenlink Communications">Suddenlink Communications</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Charter_Communications" title="Charter Communications">Charter Communications</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The Max GO <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a>, <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> app was released on August 11, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Programming">Programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movie_library">Movie library</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Movie library">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of August 2013, Cinemax – through <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> – maintains exclusive first-run film licensing agreements with network sister company <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros. Entertainment</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Animation" title="Warner Bros. Animation">Warner Bros. Animation</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Line_Cinema" title="New Line Cinema">New Line Cinema</a> since 2005, and <a href="/wiki/Castle_Rock_Entertainment" title="Castle Rock Entertainment">Castle Rock Entertainment</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and various others such as <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> since 1980 (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox_Animation" title="20th Century Fox Animation">20th Century Fox Animation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Sky_Studios" title="Blue Sky Studios">Blue Sky Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regency_Enterprises#New_Regency" title="Regency Enterprises">New Regency Productions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Searchlight_Pictures" title="Fox Searchlight Pictures">Fox Searchlight Pictures</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a> since 2003 (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Universal_Animation_Studios" title="Universal Animation Studios">Universal Animation Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Working_Title_Films" title="Working Title Films">Working Title Films</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illumination_Entertainment" title="Illumination Entertainment">Illumination Entertainment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Focus_Features" title="Focus Features">Focus Features</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Summit_Entertainment" title="Summit Entertainment">Summit Entertainment</a> since 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks" title="DreamWorks">DreamWorks</a> since 1996 (excluding films co-produced with <a href="/wiki/Touchstone_Pictures" title="Touchstone Pictures">Touchstone Pictures</a>, rights to such films are held by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_channel)" title="Showtime (TV channel)">Showtime</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first-run film output agreement with Fox was renewed by HBO for ten years on August 15, 2012 (allowing the studio to release its films through the digital platforms <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">iTunes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a> during a film's term of license with the channel for the first time)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBO_Fox_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBO_Fox-39">[39]</a></sup> and the Universal output deal was renewed for ten years on January 6, 2013 (with the exception of a few animated films that HBO can offer to pass over to the <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a> streaming service).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cinemax also shows sub-runs – runs of films that have already received broadcast or <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_syndication" title="Broadcast syndication">syndicated television</a> airings – of theatrical films from <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> (including content from subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Republic_Pictures" title="Republic Pictures">Republic Pictures</a>, both for films released prior to 1998 while <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_Movies" title="Nickelodeon Movies">Nickelodeon Movies</a> films never aired on Cinemax), <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_Motion_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures">Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>, Touchstone Pictures, <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Pictures" title="Hollywood Pictures">Hollywood Pictures</a>, and former subsidiary and current independently operated studio <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miramax_Films" title="Miramax Films">Miramax Films</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar</a> films never aired on Cinemax), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Entertainment" title="Sony Pictures Entertainment">Sony Pictures Entertainment</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Classics" title="Sony Pictures Classics">Sony Pictures Classics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Screen_Gems" title="Screen Gems">Screen Gems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/TriStar_Pictures" title="TriStar Pictures">TriStar Pictures</a>, all for films released prior to 2005, despite <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Animation" title="Sony Pictures Animation">two</a> <a href="/wiki/Stage_6_Films" title="Stage 6 Films">others</a> never aired movies on Cinemax and were <a href="/wiki/Sony_Movie_Channel" title="Sony Movie Channel">Sony Movie Channel</a> exclusive), <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a> (including content from subsidiaries <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orion_Pictures" title="Orion Pictures">Orion Pictures</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Samuel_Goldwyn_Company" title="The Samuel Goldwyn Company">The Samuel Goldwyn Company</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lions_Gate_Entertainment" title="Lions Gate Entertainment">Lions Gate Entertainment</a> (for films released prior to 2004, while films since 2004 air on other networks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Films that HBO maintains pay cable rights to will usually also run on Cinemax during the period of its term of licensing, although some feature films from the aforementioned studios to which the two channels hold broadcast rights will make their premium television debut on Cinemax several weeks before its premiere on HBO and vice versa. Cinemax rarely airs G-rated films during the morning hours, instead opting to air films with R, PG-13 or PG ratings during these time slots. The channel also produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Documentary" title="Documentary">documentary</a> films under the banner <i>Cinemax Reel Life</i>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_first-run_contracts">Former first-run contracts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Former first-run contracts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 1980s, Cinemax had broadcast films from Columbia Pictures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Movie_Duels_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Movie_Duels-42">[42]</a></sup> TriStar Pictures (both output deals of which expired after 2004) and Orion Pictures through exclusive distribution deals with HBO;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> as of February 2013, rival premium channel Starz has an exclusive film output deal with Columbia and TriStar parent Sony Pictures Entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> films released between mid-1988 and late 1997 were broadcast on Cinemax;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> rival Showtime assumed pay television rights between 1998 and 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> HBO/Cinemax's contract with <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> expired after 2012, at which time Netflix assumed pay television rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> HBO relinquished its deal with DreamWorks' live-action films at the end of 2010, when the distribution rights shifted from Paramount Pictures to Touchstone Pictures (whose films are broadcast by Showtime through a distribution agreement with the Walt Disney Motion Pictures Group).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_programming">Original programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Original programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_programs_broadcast_by_Cinemax" title="List of programs broadcast by Cinemax">List of programs broadcast by Cinemax</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Max_After_Dark">Max After Dark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Max After Dark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Max After Dark</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Late_night_television_in_the_United_States" title="Late night television in the United States">late night</a> adult <a href="/wiki/Block_programming" title="Block programming">programming block</a> that features licensed <a href="/wiki/Softcore_pornography" title="Softcore pornography">softcore pornographic</a> films and original series. Cinemax does not have set start or end times for the block, as they vary depending on the mainstream feature films – and original series on certain nights – that air prior to and following it, and also depend on the number of programs and programs in particular that are scheduled to air within the block. Programs that air under the Max After Dark banner carry either a TV-MA or R rating (usually the former), primarily for <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">strong sexual content</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nudity" title="Nudity">nudity</a>. The block has often been the subject of both scrutiny in the media and a source of humor in <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>, with references to Cinemax's late night programming being featured in various films and television shows. Because of the block's presence, Cinemax is most commonly given the jocular nickname, "Skinemax".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skinemax_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-skinemax-51">[51]</a></sup> The network itself has acknowledged this by using a play on this term for its 2011 documentary series, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Skin_to_the_Max&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Skin to the Max (page does not exist)">Skin to the Max</a></i>.</p>
<p>The late night adult series that currently air first-run episodes as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> are <i><a href="/wiki/Lingerie_(TV_series)" title="Lingerie (TV series)">Lingerie</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_on_Top" title="Life on Top">Life on Top</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Femme_Fatales_(TV_series)" title="Femme Fatales (TV series)">Femme Fatales</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zane%E2%80%99s_The_Jump_Off" title="Zane’s The Jump Off">Zane’s The Jump Off</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Working_Girls_in_Bed&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Working Girls in Bed (page does not exist)">Working Girls in Bed</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Topless_Prophet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Topless Prophet (page does not exist)">Topless Prophet</a></i> (which debuted on May 30, 2014 as the network's first <a href="/wiki/Reality_television" title="Reality television">reality series</a> and follows the world of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exotic_dancing" title="Exotic dancing">exotic dancing</a> in <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Adult films often air alongside these series, though this depends on the Cinemax multiplex channel and sometimes depending on that night's Max After Dark schedule on each channel.</p>
<p>The block originally debuted on May 4, 1984, as a weekly block called "Cinemax Friday After Dark";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> these adult programs eventually expanded to seven-night-a-week airings by the late 1990s. Cinemax maintains an on-air policy – that has been in effect since 1993 – not to air any adult programming on its main channel before 11:30 p.m. Eastern Time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinemax_makeover_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cinemax_makeover-6">[6]</a></sup> The adult programming featured on Max After Dark is not limited solely to the main Cinemax channel: MoreMax also airs softcore pornographic films and series, sometimes airing earlier (10:30 p.m. Eastern Time at the earliest) than the main Cinemax channel would allow; ActionMax, ThrillerMax and OuterMax also feature adult films on their late night schedules, even though the softcore adult films and series do not necessarily fit the respective formats of these multiplex services.</p>
<p>Conversely, MovieMax (which is aimed at families) and 5StarMax (which carries a format of largely critically acclaimed, mainstream feature films) generally do not run any adult programs because of their respective programming formats. Some of the adult films featured on the Max After Dark block also air late nights on sister channel HBO Zone, which is the only HBO multiplex channel to feature pornographic film content.</p>
<p>Cinemax is able to carry softcore pornographic programs as well as other forms of adult content within the channel's mainstream programming in part since the FCC's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watershed_(television)#United_States" title="Watershed (television)">content regulations</a> apply only to channels that broadcast on the <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_television" title="Terrestrial television"><i>publicly owned</i> spectrum</a> and not those only available on restricted-access cable networks, which have consequently taken considerably more leeway in their programming.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream_action_programming">Mainstream action programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mainstream action programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On August 12, 2011, Cinemax began airing original series other than the licensed Max After Dark programming, with the addition of prime time action-oriented series targeted at men between 18 and 49 years of age. On that date, Cinemax debuted its first mainstream original program, the U.S. premiere of the British action series <i><a href="/wiki/Strike_Back_(TV_series)" title="Strike Back (TV series)">Strike Back</a></i> (first-run episodes of the series aired by Cinemax during its 2011 season were from the show's <a href="/wiki/Strike_Back:_Project_Dawn" title="Strike Back: Project Dawn">second season</a>). The series originally debuted in 2010 on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky1" title="Sky1">Sky1</a> in the United Kingdom, which Home Box Office, Inc./Cinemax partnered with to produce the series after the conclusion of its first season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> On October 19, 2012, Cinemax launched its second primetime original series, <i><a href="/wiki/Hunted_(2012_TV_series)" title="Hunted (2012 TV series)">Hunted</a></i>, in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/BBC_One" title="BBC One">BBC One</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tvwise2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-tvwise2-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Ball_(screenwriter)" title="Alan Ball (screenwriter)">Alan Ball</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Banshee_(TV_series)" title="Banshee (TV series)">Banshee</a></i> followed in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Two new shows premiered in 2014: the Steve Kronish-produced <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sandbox_(TV_series)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sandbox (TV series) (page does not exist)">Sandbox</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Soderbergh" title="Steven Soderbergh">Steven Soderbergh</a>-produced <i><a href="/wiki/The_Knick" title="The Knick">The Knick</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other upcoming action series that are in development or set to air include <i>Hurt People</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <i>The Sixth Family</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>Kingpin</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <i>Blanco</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <i>Quarry</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <i>Signal</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <i>Trail of Blood</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and <i>Outcast</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_program_blocks">Former program blocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Former program blocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><b>Drive-In Saturday Night</b> – Running Saturday nights at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time from 1984 to 1993 (competing against a similar block, "Joe Bob's Drive-In Theater" on The Movie Channel, during that period), the "Drive-In Saturday Night" block featured a broad mix of cult films and action movies.</li>
<li><b>Cinemax Comic Relief</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1985 to 1988, showcasing a selection of four or five different comedy films each month.</li>
<li><b>Cinemax Comedy Experiment</b> – Running from February 16, 1985 to 1988, the "Cinemax Comedy Experiment" was a showcase of original comedy specials that were more free-form in style (some featuring elements of the sketch comedy and improv genre) than the conventional stand-up format (such as the first special under the banner, <i>Howie Mandel: Live at Carnegie Mall</i>).</li>
<li><b>Rising Star</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1985 to 1986, showcasing feature films from up-and-coming actors.</li>
<li><b>Cinemax Film Discovery</b> – This film block ran in varied primetime timeslots from 1985 to 1990, showcasing feature films not previously seen on television or in wide theatrical release.</li>
<li><b>Military Max</b> – Running from 1985 to 1988, this showcase featured a mix of four or five different military-themed feature films (most commonly war films, although with occasional broadcasts of military-themed films of other genres).</li>
<li><b>Cinemax Director's Chair</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1985 to 1993, showcasing a selection of notable films from an acclaimed director.</li>
<li><b>Cinemax Western Roundup</b> – Running from 1985 to 1993, this weekend block featured a mix of film <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">westerns</a>.</li>
<li><b>Cinemania</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1988 to 1993, showcasing a selection of comedic films.</li>
<li><b>Cinemax Classic Collection</b> – Running from 1988 to 1992, this block showcased a selection of film classics from the 1930s to the 1960s.</li>
<li><b>Cinemax From the Heart</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1988 to 1993, showcasing a selection of romantic comedies and dramas.</li>
<li><b>Vanguard Cinema</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1988 to 1993, showcasing a selection of critically acclaimed films from the United States and abroad (the block was divided into three sub-blocks: "Cinemax Documentary," featuring first-run documentary films; "New Wave Films," featuring acclaimed recent movies; and "Cinemax Imports," originally a standalone block that began in 1985, which showcased film releases from other countries).</li>
<li><b>Max Crime Time</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1988 to 1997, showcasing a selection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crime_drama" title="Crime drama">crime dramas</a>, detective films or <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">film noirs</a>.</li>
<li><b>Starring...</b> – This film block ran in varied timeslots from 1988 to 1997, showcasing a selection of hits as well as some early films from a particular film actor throughout the month.</li>
<li><b>Summer of 1000 Movies</b> – "The Summer of 1000 Movies" annual <a href="/wiki/Film_festival" title="Film festival">film festival</a> ran from 1992 to 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and ran until the mid-2000s, in which the channel ran 1,000 films (many with a similar subject) over the course of each summer without repeats.</li>
<li><b>Max Hits at 8</b> – Running from 1997 to 2001, this block showcased a popular feature film each night at 8:00 p.m. Eastern Time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaxMovieFan_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaxMovieFan-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Max Prime at 10/Max Time</b> – Debuting in 1997 under the "Max Prime" banner, this block showcased a particular choice movie of various genres each night at 10:00 p.m. Eastern Time (or on weeknights only between 1998 and 2000, as a result of the creation of the "ActionMax on Cinemax" and "ThrillerMax on Cinemax" blocks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaxMovieFan_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-MaxMovieFan-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Branding">Branding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Branding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-overly_detailed" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may contain an excessive amount of <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_an_indiscriminate_collection_of_information" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">intricate detail</a> that may only interest a specific audience</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Content_forking#Article_spinoffs:_.22Summary_style.22_meta-articles_and_summary_sections" title="Wikipedia:Content forking">spinning off</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Handling_trivia#Recommendations_for_handling_trivia" title="Wikipedia:Handling trivia">relocating</a> any relevant information, and removing excessive detail that may be against <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">Wikipedia's inclusion policy</a>.</span> <small><i>(September 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The logo originally used by from the channel's 1980 launch featured the Cinemax name in Avant Garde type on a semi-circular rectangle (this logo was jocularly referred to by network management as resembling a "flying <a href="/wiki/Dildo" title="Dildo">dildo</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>); the "Coming Up Next" bumpers and graphics were similar to parent network HBO's graphics of the concurring time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The channel phased in a new logo in 1985, featuring seven <a href="/wiki/Rhomboid" title="Rhomboid">rhomboids</a> sized to fit each letter of the channel's name (rendered in a lowercase, bold and italicized <a href="/wiki/Univers" title="Univers">Univers Condensed</a> type), variants of the logo used different coloring; this logo was used in print ads and during <a href="/wiki/Bumper_(broadcasting)" title="Bumper (broadcasting)">bumpers</a> for a short time as the original 1980 opening bumpers were still being used, until a new set of feature presentation opens debuted in the spring of 1985.</p>
<p>In 1997, the network implemented a new logo rendered in lowercase <a href="/wiki/Impact_(typeface)" title="Impact (typeface)">Impact</a> type with a circle highlighting the 'max' (as with Showtime's highlighting of 'SHO' in their logo, the use of 'MAX' as the logo focal point comes from the channel's former <i><a href="/wiki/TV_Guide" title="TV Guide">TV Guide</a></i> abbreviation in the magazine's local listings era). Slight modifications of the logo's coloring were made during this period; the logo was often shown with just the circle 'max'.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cinemax.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="126" data-file-width="349" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Cinemax.svg/165px-Cinemax.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Cinemax.svg/248px-Cinemax.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Cinemax.svg/330px-Cinemax.svg.png 2x" width="165"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cinemax.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alternate version of current logo used since August 1, 2011, seen primarily on Cinemax.com.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In February 2008, a new minimalized branding campaign was introduced, with voiceovers for movie promotions and ratings bumpers dropped from all of the Cinemax networks. The promotions featured <a href="/wiki/Adult_Swim" title="Adult Swim">Adult Swim</a>-style introductions with white text on black screens, while "up next" bumpers just featured the film title and lead actors set to only sound effects and short music clips instead of full interstitial music. All channel logos were redesigned; the main Cinemax channel in particular became visually referred to as simply "max" – though cast members from the network's "After Dark" series continued to refer the network verbally as "Cinemax", and ads seen on other television stations and cable networks promoting the channel continued to use the original 1997 logo design (though a variant without the circle behind the 'max' was used from 2010 to 2011).</p>
<p>In August 2011, Cinemax introduced a new logo in line with promotional efforts for <i>Strike Back</i> – a vertically tilted yellow rhomboid with uppercase black "CINEMAX" lettering (a logo variant with inverted coloring also exists). Only the "MAX" portion is used for Cinemax on Demand and Max Go (for both services, an additional rhomboid is added next to the yellow/black "MAX" to fit either "OD" [for "on demand"] or "GO"), along with the linear multiplex channels – to which the prefix titles for each channel are added before it without a background. It was originally unveiled in on-air promos for its upcoming original programming, on its <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> accounts and on its <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> channel in May 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The official website and Max Go continued to use the 2008 logo variant until August 11, 2011, when both sites were extensively redesigned. Despite the rebrand, Cinemax's multiplex channels (with the exception of the main channel and MoreMax, which do not use any on-screen watermarking whatsoever) confusingly continued to feature logo bugs using their variants of the 1997 logo during films and other programs until 2013.</p>
<p>In July 2016, five years later, CineMax and its multiplex channels launched their latest graphics, with the current logo changing from yellow and black to black and white, which was unveiled in April 2016 in the tonight bumpers, and some promotion. Unlike the 2011 logos, these ones are not silent, and do not appear in an alternate yellow background. So instead the use a camera shutter click on each channel with the outline appearing first then the inside is covered in white, then right after, the follow signs (Facebook, Twitter, and Instagram) are shown. Although this has happened, CineMax will continue to feature the 2011 logo on the copyright bumper, the rating/content bumpers, and on the official website.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Network_slogans">Network slogans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cinemax&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Network slogans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<ul>
<li><b>1980–1982:</b> <i><u>The</u> (24-Hour) Movie Network</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1982–1983:</b> <i>We Are Your Movie Star</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1983–1984:</b> <i>Our Difference is Our Movies</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1984–1986:</b> <i>We Take You to the Stars</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1986–1989:</b> <i>Entertainment to the Max</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1986–1988:</b> <i>It's Max</i></li>
<li><b>1988–1989:</b> <i>See the Light, See Cinemax</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1989–1991:</b> <i>More Movies, More Choice</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1989–1997:</b> <i>Cinemax, The Most Movies on Pay TV</i> <small>(used as a secondary slogan)</small></li>
<li><b>1991–1993:</b> <i>Cinemax, If You Like Movies</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<ul>
<li><b>1993–1997:</b> <i>The Best Network for Movies on TV</i></li>
<li><b>1997–2000:</b> <i>The Movie Service for the Movie Fan</i></li>
<li><b>2000–2003:</b> <i>Best! Most! Max!</i></li>
<li><b>2003–2008</b>: <i>Take It to the Movies</i></li>
<li><b>2008–2011:</b> <i>Take It to the Max</i></li>
<li><b>2011–2013:</b> <i>We Are Your Movie Channel</i></li>
<li><b>2013–2016:</b> <i>Max. In Movies.</i></li>
<li><b>2016-present:</b> <i>Follow Us On Cinemax</i></li>
</ul>
</td>
